<p><br/>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Road_running">Road running</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Road running">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Road_running" title="Road running">Road running</a></div>
<p>Road running competitions are running events (predominantly long distance) which are mainly conducted on courses of <a href="/wiki/Road_surface" title="Road surface">paved</a> or <a href="/wiki/Asphalt_concrete" title="Asphalt concrete">tarmac</a> <a href="/wiki/Road" title="Road">roads</a>, although major events often finish on the track of a main <a href="/wiki/Stadium" title="Stadium">stadium</a>. In addition to being a common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recreational_sport" title="Recreational sport">recreational sport</a>, the elite level of the sport – particularly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marathon_race" title="Marathon race">marathon races</a> – are one of the most popular aspects of athletics. Road racing events can be of virtually any distance, but the most common and well known are the <a href="/wiki/Marathon" title="Marathon">marathon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Half_marathon" title="Half marathon">half marathon</a>, <a href="/wiki/10K_run" title="10K run">10 km</a> and <a href="/wiki/5K_run" title="5K run">5 km</a>. The marathon is the only road running event featured at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAAF_World_Championships_in_Athletics" title="IAAF World Championships in Athletics">IAAF World Championships in Athletics</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summer_Olympics" title="Summer Olympics">Summer Olympics</a>, although there is also an annual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAAF_World_Half_Marathon_Championships" title="IAAF World Half Marathon Championships">IAAF World Half Marathon Championships</a>. The marathon is also the only road running event featured at the <a href="/wiki/World_Para_Athletics_Championships" title="World Para Athletics Championships">World Para Athletics Championships</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Summer_Paralympic_Games" title="Summer Paralympic Games">Summer Paralympics</a>. The <a href="/wiki/World_Marathon_Majors" title="World Marathon Majors">World Marathon Majors</a> series includes the six most prestigious marathon competitions at the elite level – the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Marathon" title="Berlin Marathon">Berlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boston_Marathon" title="Boston Marathon">Boston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Marathon" title="Chicago Marathon">Chicago</a>, <a href="/wiki/London_Marathon" title="London Marathon">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Marathon" title="New York City Marathon">New York City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Marathon" title="Tokyo Marathon">Tokyo Marathons</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NationalMarathonDC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/NationalMarathonDC.jpg/220px-NationalMarathonDC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/NationalMarathonDC.jpg/330px-NationalMarathonDC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/NationalMarathonDC.jpg/440px-NationalMarathonDC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NationalMarathonDC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Runners in the popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Marathon" title="National Marathon">National Marathon</a> race in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a></div></div></div>
<p>The sport of road running finds its roots in the activities of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Footmen" title="Footmen">footmen</a>: male servants who ran alongside the carriages of <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy_(class)" title="Aristocracy (class)">aristocrats</a> around the 18th century, and who also ran errands over distances for their masters. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foot_racing" title="Foot racing">Foot racing</a> competitions evolved from <a href="/wiki/Gambling" title="Gambling">wagers</a> between aristocrats, who pitted their footman against that of another aristocrat in order to determine a winner. The sport became <a href="/wiki/Professional_sports" title="Professional sports">professionalised</a> as footmen were hired specifically on their athletic ability and began to devote their lives to training for the gambling events. The <a href="/wiki/Amateur_sports" title="Amateur sports">amateur sports</a> movement in the late 19th century marginalised competitions based on the professional, gambling model. The <a href="/wiki/1896_Summer_Olympics" title="1896 Summer Olympics">1896 Summer Olympics</a> saw the birth of the modern marathon and the event led to the growth of road running competitions through annual events such as the Boston Marathon (first held in 1897) and the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Biwa_Marathon" title="Lake Biwa Marathon">Lake Biwa Marathon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fukuoka_Marathon" title="Fukuoka Marathon">Fukuoka Marathons</a>, which were established in the 1940s. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Running_boom" title="Running boom">1970s running boom</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> made road running a common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastime" title="Pastime">pastime</a> and also increased its popularity at the elite level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ekiden" title="Ekiden">Ekiden</a> contests – which originated in <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> and remain very popular there – are a relay race variation on the marathon, being in contrast to the typically individual sport of road running.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cross_country_running">Cross country running</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cross country running">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cross_country_running" title="Cross country running">Cross country running</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roy_Griak_Invitational-20070929.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="678" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Roy_Griak_Invitational-20070929.jpg/220px-Roy_Griak_Invitational-20070929.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Roy_Griak_Invitational-20070929.jpg/330px-Roy_Griak_Invitational-20070929.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Roy_Griak_Invitational-20070929.jpg/440px-Roy_Griak_Invitational-20070929.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roy_Griak_Invitational-20070929.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Competitors mid-race at a boys high school event in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a></div></div></div>
<p>Cross country running is the most naturalistic of the sports in athletics as competitions take place on open-air courses over surfaces such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grass" title="Grass">grass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodland</a> trails, and <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">earth</a>. It is both an individual and <a href="/wiki/Team_sport" title="Team sport">team sport</a>, as runners are judged on an individual basis and a points scoring method is used for teams. Competitions are typically long distance races of 3 km (1.9 mi) or more which are usually held in autumn and winter. Cross country's most successful athletes often compete in long-distance track and road events as well.
</p><p>The <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Crick_Run&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Crick Run (page does not exist)">Crick Run</a> in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> in 1838 was the first recorded instance of an organised cross country competition. The sport gained popularity in British, then American schools in the 19th century and culminated in the creation of the first <a href="/wiki/International_Cross_Country_Championships" title="International Cross Country Championships">International Cross Country Championships</a> in 1903.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The annual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAAF_World_Cross_Country_Championships" title="IAAF World Cross Country Championships">IAAF World Cross Country Championships</a> was inaugurated in 1973 and this remains the highest level of competition for the sport. A number of continental cross country competitions are held, with championships taking place in <a href="/wiki/Asian_Cross_Country_Championships" title="Asian Cross Country Championships">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Cross_Country_Championships" title="European Cross Country Championships">Europe</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Americas_Cross_Country_Championships" title="Americas Cross Country Championships">North America</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_American_Cross_Country_Championships" title="South American Cross Country Championships">South America</a>. The sport has retained its status at the scholastic level, particularly in the United Kingdom and United States. At the professional level, the foremost competitions come under the banner of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAAF_Cross_Country_Permit_Meetings" title="IAAF Cross Country Permit Meetings">IAAF Cross Country Permit Meetings</a>.
</p><p>While cross country competitions are no longer held at the Olympics, having featured in the athletics programme from 1912 to 1924, it has been present as one of the events within the <a href="/wiki/Modern_pentathlon" title="Modern pentathlon">modern pentathlon</a> competition since the <a href="/wiki/1912_Summer_Olympics" title="1912 Summer Olympics">1912 Summer Olympics</a>. One variation on traditional cross country is <a href="/wiki/Mountain_running" title="Mountain running">mountain running</a>, which incorporates significant uphill and/or downhill sections as an additional challenge to the course. <a href="/wiki/Fell_running" title="Fell running">Fell running</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orienteering" title="Orienteering">Orienteering</a> are other competitive sports similar to cross country, although they feature an element of <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">navigation</a> which is absent from the set courses of cross country.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Racewalking">Racewalking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Racewalking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Racewalking" title="Racewalking">Racewalking</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1912_Athletics_men%27s_10_kilometre_walk.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="1068" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/1912_Athletics_men%27s_10_kilometre_walk.JPG/220px-1912_Athletics_men%27s_10_kilometre_walk.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/1912_Athletics_men%27s_10_kilometre_walk.JPG/330px-1912_Athletics_men%27s_10_kilometre_walk.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/1912_Athletics_men%27s_10_kilometre_walk.JPG/440px-1912_Athletics_men%27s_10_kilometre_walk.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1912_Athletics_men%27s_10_kilometre_walk.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A track-side judge monitoring technique at the <a href="/wiki/1912_Summer_Olympics" title="1912 Summer Olympics">1912 Summer Olympics</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Racewalking is a form of competitive <a href="/wiki/Walking" title="Walking">walking</a> that usually takes place on open-air roads, although running tracks are also occasionally used. Racewalking is the only sport in athletics in which judges monitor athletes on their technique. Racewalkers must always have a foot in contact with the ground and their advancing leg must be straightened, not bent at the knee – failure to follow these rules results in disqualification from the race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAAFRaceW_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAAFRaceW-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Racewalking finds its roots in the sport of <a href="/wiki/Pedestrianism" title="Pedestrianism">pedestrianism</a> which emerged in the late 18th century in England. Spectators would gamble on the outcome of the walking competitions. The sport took on an endurance aspect and competitions were held over long distances or walkers would have to achieve a certain distance within a specified time frame, such as <a href="/wiki/Centurion_(racewalking)" title="Centurion (racewalking)">Centurion</a> contests of walking 100 miles (160 km) within 24 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAAFRaceW_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-IAAFRaceW-32">[32]</a></sup> During this period, racewalking was frequently held on athletics tracks for ease of measurement, and the <a href="/wiki/1908_Summer_Olympics" title="1908 Summer Olympics">1908 Summer Olympics</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> saw the introduction of the 3500-metre and 10-mile walks. Racewalking was briefly dropped from the Olympic programme in <a href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_1928_Summer_Olympics" title="Athletics at the 1928 Summer Olympics">1928</a>, but the men's <a href="/wiki/50_kilometres_race_walk" title="50 kilometres race walk">50 kilometres race walk</a> has been held at every Olympic Games but one since 1932. The men's <a href="/wiki/20_kilometres_race_walk" title="20 kilometres race walk">20 kilometres race walk</a> was added to the Olympic athletics schedule in 1956 and the women's event was first held in 1992. The most common events in modern competition are over 10 km, 20 km and 50 km on roads, although women's 3 km and men's 5 km are held on indoor tracks.
</p><p>The highest level racewalking competitions occur at the IAAF World Championships in Athletics and at the Summer Olympics, although the sport also has its own separate major competition – the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAAF_World_Race_Walking_Cup" title="IAAF World Race Walking Cup">IAAF World Race Walking Cup</a> – which has been held since 1961. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAAF_World_Race_Walking_Challenge" title="IAAF World Race Walking Challenge">IAAF World Race Walking Challenge</a> forms the primary seasonal competition – athletes earn points for their performances at ten selected racewalking competitions and the highest scoring walkers are entered into that year's <a href="/wiki/IAAF_Race_Walking_Challenge_Final" title="IAAF Race Walking Challenge Final">IAAF Race Walking Challenge Final</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Categories">Categories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Categories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Foekje_Dillema.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2600" data-file-width="2105" decoding="async" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Foekje_Dillema.jpg/220px-Foekje_Dillema.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Foekje_Dillema.jpg/330px-Foekje_Dillema.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Foekje_Dillema.jpg/440px-Foekje_Dillema.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Foekje_Dillema.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Foekje_Dillema" title="Foekje Dillema">Foekje Dillema</a> was banned from the women's division in 1950</div></div></div>
<p>The significant variation in people's abilities in the sport of athletics has led to the creation of numerous competitive categories, in order that athletes are pitted against rivals of a similar kind or ability, and to include groups of people who would otherwise not be competitive in open-to-all events. The eligibility of athletes for a given category is occasionally a source of controversy among the sport's participants, officials and spectators, with disputes typically being rooted in deliberate <a href="/wiki/Cheating" title="Cheating">cheating</a> in order to gain a competitive advantage or differing cultural perspectives over the eligibility of a category.
</p><p>Beyond the primary categories based on physical attributes, some competitions have further eligibility criteria based on nationality, community membership or occupation.
</p>
<h3><span id="Men.27s_and_women.27s_divisions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Men's_and_women's_divisions"><span class="anchor" id="Women"></span>Men's and women's divisions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Men's and women's divisions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The foremost division of this kind is by sex[ling]: in athletics, men and women almost exclusively compete against people of the same sex. In contrast to the men's division, the development of the women's division has caused regular dispute in terms of eligibility. Several <a href="/wiki/Intersex" title="Intersex">intersex</a> athletes had success in the women's division in the early 20th century, such as <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82awa_Walasiewicz" title="Stanisława Walasiewicz">Stanisława Walasiewicz</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_Weston" title="Mary Weston">Mary Weston</a> (later Mark), and the IAAF responded by introducing <a href="/wiki/Sex_verification_in_sports" title="Sex verification in sports">sex verification</a> for all athletes in the women's category, beginning with the disqualification of sprinter <a href="/wiki/Foekje_Dillema" title="Foekje Dillema">Foekje Dillema</a> in 1950 after she refused to be tested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Olympic champion <a href="/wiki/Ewa_K%C5%82obukowska" title="Ewa Kłobukowska">Ewa Kłobukowska</a> became the first athlete to publicly fail the test in 1967 and the humiliation she suffered as a result of the announcement led to sex tests becoming a confidential process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Hurdler <a href="/wiki/Maria_Jos%C3%A9_Mart%C3%ADnez-Pati%C3%B1o" title="Maria José Martínez-Patiño">Maria José Martínez-Patiño</a> failed a test and was disqualified in 1985, but publicly fought the ban in court and was reinstated in 1988. In 1991, the IAAF replaced the sex <a href="/wiki/Chromatin" title="Chromatin">chromatin</a> test with general medical tests for athletes of all divisions, due to changes in ethical and scientific viewpoints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>The question of eligibility for the women's division continued to be a contentious and public issue into the 21st century, with <a href="/wiki/Caster_Semenya" title="Caster Semenya">Caster Semenya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dutee_Chand" title="Dutee Chand">Dutee Chand</a> bearing periods of inegibility and taking the IAAF to the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Arbitration_for_Sport" title="Court of Arbitration for Sport">Court of Arbitration for Sport</a> over their bans under the <a href="/wiki/Hyperandrogenism" title="Hyperandrogenism">hyperandrogenism</a> rules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> An increasing number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans_men" title="Trans men">trans men</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans_women" title="Trans women">trans women</a> began to compete in the women's division in the 2010s, which caused other athletes in the division to raise questions of fairness in competition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>The dispute reached new heights in 2019 with the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Human_Rights_Council" title="United Nations Human Rights Council">United Nations Human Rights Council</a> issuing a statement that the IAAF was breaching "international human rights norms and standards" through its practice of allowing some athletes to compete in the women's division only once they had lowered their testosterone levels through medical intervention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The IAAF and several prominent women athletes, such as <a href="/wiki/Paula_Radcliffe" title="Paula Radcliffe">Paula Radcliffe</a>, said this was required in order to prevent a situation where countries deliberately sought out athletes who were intersex, transgender or had a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Difference_in_sex_development" title="Difference in sex development">difference in sex development</a> in order to succeed in women's sport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Others have argued for the abolition of gender verification testing, with academic Maren Behrensen citing the harm to tested athletes' social and emotional well-being, the inaccuracy of the medical tests, the difficulty of determining the exact performance advantage provided by a given condition, and the moral risk of "gender-engineering" by setting a biological definition for a female athlete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age">Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Masters_athletics" title="Masters athletics">Masters athletics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fauja_Singh_in_2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="604" data-file-width="453" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Fauja_Singh_in_2007.jpg/150px-Fauja_Singh_in_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Fauja_Singh_in_2007.jpg/225px-Fauja_Singh_in_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Fauja_Singh_in_2007.jpg/300px-Fauja_Singh_in_2007.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fauja_Singh_in_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Masters marathon runner <a href="/wiki/Fauja_Singh" title="Fauja Singh">Fauja Singh</a></div></div></div>
<p>Age is a significant determiner of ability to compete in athletics, with athletic ability generally increasing through childhood and adolescence, peaking in early adulthood, then gradually declining from around the age of 30 onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> As a result, numerous age categories have been created to encourage younger and older athletes to engage in competition. At international level, there are three major categories for young athletes: <a href="/wiki/Under-23_athletics" title="Under-23 athletics">under-23</a>, <a href="/wiki/Under-20_athletics" title="Under-20 athletics">under-20</a> (formerly junior), and <a href="/wiki/Under-18_athletics" title="Under-18 athletics">under-18</a> (formerly youth). Beyond international rules, different youth categories are in use in the sport, often in the form of two-year or single age groupings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWAge_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-AWAge-44">[44]</a></sup> Age categories are more extensive for older athletes and these are commonly organised under the umbrella of <a href="/wiki/Masters_athletics" title="Masters athletics">masters athletics</a>, which has age groups spanning five years for all athletes aged 35 and above. There is no limit to the number of age groupings, hence <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_Kowalski" title="Stanisław Kowalski">Stanisław Kowalski</a> holds a world record for men aged 105 years and over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> For competitions where age is not taken into account, this is known as senior or <a href="/wiki/Open_class_(track_and_field)" title="Open class (track and field)">open class</a> athletics; in international rules there remain some restrictions on younger people competing in endurance events for health reasons .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWAge_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-AWAge-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Athletes' eligibility for a competitive age grouping is typically assessed through official documentation, such as birth records or passports. Instances of <a href="/wiki/Age_fabrication" title="Age fabrication">age cheating</a> have occurred at all of the IAAF's global age category championships. One prominent incident was Olympic medalist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Longosiwa" title="Thomas Longosiwa">Thomas Longosiwa</a>, who provided a falsified passport to compete at the <a href="/wiki/2006_World_Junior_Championships_in_Athletics" title="2006 World Junior Championships in Athletics">2006 World Junior Championships in Athletics</a> at age 24.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Age falsification for youth categories is mostly limited to less developed parts of the world, such as Africa and South Asia, which have less stringent controls on official documentation and many mature athletes engaging in high school competition due to disruptions to education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The same regions of the world also present issues with age verification in masters age categories, with examples such as Indian distance runners <a href="/wiki/Dharampal_Singh_Gudha" title="Dharampal Singh Gudha">Dharampal Singh Gudha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fauja_Singh" title="Fauja Singh">Fauja Singh</a> (both claiming to be over 100 years old) reaching mainstream attention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Athletes_with_disabilities">Athletes with disabilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Athletes with disabilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paralympic_athletics" title="Paralympic athletics">Paralympic athletics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shiran_Yu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1054" data-file-width="703" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Shiran_Yu.jpg/150px-Shiran_Yu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Shiran_Yu.jpg/225px-Shiran_Yu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Shiran_Yu.jpg/300px-Shiran_Yu.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shiran_Yu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Competitor in a <a href="/wiki/Wheelchair_racing" title="Wheelchair racing">wheelchair race</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_2008_Summer_Paralympics" title="Athletics at the 2008 Summer Paralympics">2008 Summer Paralympics</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Athletes with <a href="/wiki/Physical_disability" title="Physical disability">physical disabilities</a> have competed at separate international events since 1952. The <a href="/wiki/International_Paralympic_Committee" title="International Paralympic Committee">International Paralympic Committee</a> governs the competitions in athletics, and hosts the <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_Games" title="Paralympic Games">Paralympic Games</a>, which have continued since <a href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_1960_Summer_Paralympics" title="Athletics at the 1960 Summer Paralympics">1960</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ACanada_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-ACanada-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipc_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-ipc-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Competitors at elite level competitions, are classified by disability, to arrange athletes with a similar disability in the same event. A classified T12 athlete for example, is a track athlete with a visual impairment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>F = Field athletes</li>
<li>T = Track athletes</li>
<li>11–13 – <a href="/wiki/Visual_impairment" title="Visual impairment">visual impairment</a>. Compete with a <a href="/wiki/Sighted_guide" title="Sighted guide">sighted guide</a>.</li>
<li>20 – Intellectual disability</li>
<li>31–38 – <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_palsy" title="Cerebral palsy">cerebral palsy</a></li>
<li>40–46 – <a href="/wiki/Amputation" title="Amputation">amputation</a>, and others (including athletes with <a href="/wiki/Dwarfism" title="Dwarfism">dwarfism</a>)</li>
<li>51–58 – Wheelchair</li></ul>
<p>Operating independently of the Paralympic movement, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deaf" title="Deaf">deaf</a> athletes have a long-established tradition of organised athletics, with the first major world competition being included at the 1924 <a href="/wiki/Deaflympics" title="Deaflympics">Deaflympics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The primary impediments to the inclusion of deaf athletes in mainstream athletics are sound based elements of the sport, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starter%27s_pistol" title="Starter's pistol">starter's pistol</a>. This can be a disadvantage even in Paralympic sport, as shown in by the example of <a href="/wiki/Olivia_Breen" title="Olivia Breen">Olivia Breen</a> who failed to hear a false start in a cerebral palsy class race at the 2012 Paralympics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Wheelchair_racing" title="Wheelchair racing">wheelchair racing</a> athletes compete in lightweight racing chairs.  Most major <a href="/wiki/Marathon" title="Marathon">marathons</a> have wheelchair divisions and the elite racers consistently beat the runners on foot. The speed of wheel chair racers has caused difficulties for race organisers in properly staggering their start times compared to runners. A collision between Josh Cassidy (a wheelchair racer) and Tiki Gelana (a leading female marathoner) at the <a href="/wiki/2013_London_Marathon" title="2013 London Marathon">2013 London Marathon</a> brought the issue into the spotlight again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Occasionally, athletes with a disability reach a level at which they can compete against able-bodied athletes.  Legally blind <a href="/wiki/Marla_Runyan" title="Marla Runyan">Marla Runyan</a> ran in the 2000 and 2004 Olympics and won a gold medal in the 1500 metres at the <a href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_1999_Pan_American_Games" title="Athletics at the 1999 Pan American Games">1999 Pan American Games</a>.  <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Pistorius" title="Oscar Pistorius">Oscar Pistorius</a>, a double amputee, was a semi-finalist at the <a href="/wiki/2011_World_Championships_in_Athletics" title="2011 World Championships in Athletics">2011 World Championships</a> and won a silver medal as part of South Africa's <a href="/wiki/2011_World_Championships_in_Athletics_%E2%80%93_Men%27s_4_%C3%97_400_metres_relay" title="2011 World Championships in Athletics – Men's 4 × 400 metres relay">4 × 400 metres relay team</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Masters_athletics" title="Masters athletics">masters athletics</a> it is far more common to make an accommodation for athletes with a disability.  Blind <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Granstrom" title="Ivy Granstrom">Ivy Granstrom</a> set numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_records_in_masters_athletics" title="World records in masters athletics">Masters world records</a> while being guided around the track.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>The disability categories have caused dispute among athletes, with some athletes being accused of exaggerating their level of disability in order to compete in less challenging categories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Athletes with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intellectual_disabilities" title="Intellectual disabilities">intellectual disabilities</a> were banned from competition in all Paralymic sports in response to verification issues and <a href="/wiki/Cheating_at_the_Paralympic_Games" title="Cheating at the Paralympic Games">cheating at the 2000 Summer Paralympics</a> and the intellectual disability athletics programme was only restored twelve years later at the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Paralympics" title="2012 Summer Paralympics">2012 Summer Paralympics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Venues">Venues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Venues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Professional athletics almost exclusively takes place in one of three types of venue: <a href="/wiki/Stadium" title="Stadium">stadiums</a>, set courses on grass or woodland, and road-based courses. Such venues ensure that events take place in a relatively standardised manner, as well as improving the safety of athletes and enjoyment for spectators. At a more basic level, many forms of athletics demand very little in terms of venue requirements; almost any open space or area of field can provide a suitable venue for basic running, jumping and throwing competitions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Track_and_field_stadium">Track and field stadium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Track and field stadium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piste_athl%C3%A9tisme-fr.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="690" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Piste_athl%C3%A9tisme-fr.svg/220px-Piste_athl%C3%A9tisme-fr.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Piste_athl%C3%A9tisme-fr.svg/330px-Piste_athl%C3%A9tisme-fr.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Piste_athl%C3%A9tisme-fr.svg/440px-Piste_athl%C3%A9tisme-fr.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piste_athl%C3%A9tisme-fr.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical layout of an outdoor track and field stadium</div></div></div>
<p>A standard outdoor track is in the shape of a <a href="/wiki/Stadium_(geometry)" title="Stadium (geometry)">stadium</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> 400 metres in length, and has at least eight lanes 1.22 m in width (small arenas might have six lanes). Older track facilities may have nonstandard track lengths, such as 440 yards (402.3 m; 1/4 mile) (common in the United States). Historically, tracks were covered by a dirt running surface. Modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All-weather_running_track" title="All-weather running track">All-weather running tracks</a> are covered by a synthetic weather-resistant running surface, which typically consists of rubber (either black SBR or colored EPDM granules), bound by polyurethane or latex resins. Older tracks may be <a href="/wiki/Cinder_track" title="Cinder track">cinder</a>-covered.
</p><p>A standard indoor track is designed similarly to an outdoor track, but is only 200 metres in length and has between four and eight lanes, each with width between 0.90 m and 1.10 m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Often, the bends of an indoor track will be banked to compensate for the small turning radius. However, because of space limitations, indoor tracks may have other nonstandard lengths, such as 160-yard (146.3 m) indoor track at <a href="/wiki/Madison_Square_Garden" title="Madison Square Garden">Madison Square Garden</a> used for the <a href="/wiki/Millrose_Games" title="Millrose Games">Millrose Games</a>. Because of space limitations, meetings held at indoor facilities do not hold many of athletics events typically contested outdoors.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cross_country_courses">Cross country courses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cross country courses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Armedforces_crosscountry_coppingerandballas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Armedforces_crosscountry_coppingerandballas.jpg/220px-Armedforces_crosscountry_coppingerandballas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Armedforces_crosscountry_coppingerandballas.jpg/330px-Armedforces_crosscountry_coppingerandballas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Armedforces_crosscountry_coppingerandballas.jpg/440px-Armedforces_crosscountry_coppingerandballas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Armedforces_crosscountry_coppingerandballas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cross country race taking place at a snowy park in the United States.</div></div></div>
<p>There is no standardised form of cross country course and each venue is significantly defined by the environment it contains – some may be relatively flat and featureless, while others may be more challenging with natural obstacles, tight turns, and undulating ground. While a small number of purpose-built courses exist, the vast majority of cross country running courses are created by cordoning a specific area within any open natural land, typically a <a href="/wiki/Park" title="Park">park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodland</a> or <a href="/wiki/Open_space_reserve" title="Open space reserve">greenspace</a> near a <a href="/wiki/Human_settlement" title="Human settlement">settlement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XCRules_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-XCRules-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the elite and professional level, courses must be looped and each lap must be between 1750 m and 2000 m in length. Severe obstacles such as deep ditches, high barriers and thick undergrowth not normally present; the course should be able to be completed whilst remaining on foot throughout. In order to maintain the sport's distinction from road running, the usage of unnatural or <a href="/wiki/Macadam" title="Macadam">macadamised</a> surfaces is generally kept to a minimum or avoided entirely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-XCRules_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-XCRules-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because the majority of races take place on areas of grass, soil, mud or earth, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weather_conditions" title="Weather conditions">weather conditions</a> can significantly affect the difficulty of cross country courses, as <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a> reduces traction and can create areas of standing water.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Road_courses">Road courses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Road courses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:10k_run_Toronto_May_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/10k_run_Toronto_May_2010.jpg/220px-10k_run_Toronto_May_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/10k_run_Toronto_May_2010.jpg/330px-10k_run_Toronto_May_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/10k_run_Toronto_May_2010.jpg/440px-10k_run_Toronto_May_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:10k_run_Toronto_May_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical road running course on the inner-city roads of <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a></div></div></div>
<p>The surface of road races is highly important and the IAAF dictate that the courses must be along man-made roads, bicycle paths or footpaths. Courses set along major roads of cities are typical of road running events, and <a href="/wiki/Traffic" title="Traffic">traffic</a> is usually cordoned off from the area during the competition. While soft ground, such as grass, is generally avoided, races may start and finish on soft ground or within a track and field stadium. Road racing courses come in two primary types: looped and point-to-point. Courses may be measured and designed to cover a standardised distance, such as 10 km (6.2 mi), or they may simply follow a set route between two landmarks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RRRules_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-RRRules-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Road running courses over 5 km usually offer drinks or refreshment stations for runners at designated points alongside the course and medical professionals are present at the courses of major races due to the health risks involved with long-distance running.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RRRules_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-RRRules-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elite road walks are conducted on closed loop courses (usually loops of 2,000 or 2,500 meters). Refreshment stations are also present over long distance walking competitions, with drinks being available on every lap for races longer than 10 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RWRules_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-RWRules-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organizations">Organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1912, the formation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_international_sport_federations" title="List of international sport federations">international governing body</a> for athletics, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Amateur_Athletics_Federation" title="International Amateur Athletics Federation">International Amateur Athletics Federation</a>, began. In recognition of the movement of the sport from <a href="/wiki/Amateur_sports" title="Amateur sports">amateurism</a> towards <a href="/wiki/Professional_sports" title="Professional sports">professionalism</a> that began in the late 1970s, the word <i>amateur</i> was dropped from the name, and the organization was rebranded as the International Association of Athletics Federations in 2001. In late 2019, another rebranding began, with <b>World Athletics</b> as the new title of the governing body.
</p><p>World Athletics has 215 member nations and territories, which are divided into six continental areas (or area associations).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The six association areas are for <a href="/wiki/Asian_Athletics_Association" title="Asian Athletics Association">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_African_Athletics" title="Confederation of African Athletics">Africa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Athletics_Association" title="European Athletics Association">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceania_Athletics_Association" title="Oceania Athletics Association">Oceania</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_America,_Central_America_and_Caribbean_Athletic_Association" title="North America, Central America and Caribbean Athletic Association">North America</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_American_Athletics_Confederation" title="South American Athletics Confederation">South America</a>. The sports within athletics do not have their own independent governing bodies at either international or continental level; instead, all fall under the athletics authorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chap2_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chap2-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Athletics_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1357" decoding="async" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/World_Athletics_map.png/250px-World_Athletics_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/World_Athletics_map.png/375px-World_Athletics_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/World_Athletics_map.png/500px-World_Athletics_map.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_Athletics_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the six continental federations of World Athletics</div></div></div>
<ul><li><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FF00FF; color:black;"> </span> </span>  AAA – <a href="/wiki/Asian_Athletics_Association" title="Asian Athletics Association">Asian Athletics Association</a></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FF813E; color:black;"> </span> </span>  CAA – <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_African_Athletics" title="Confederation of African Athletics">Confederation of African Athletics</a></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#00FF81; color:black;"> </span> </span>  CONSUDATLE – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_American_Athletics_Confederation" title="South American Athletics Confederation">South American Athletics Confederation</a></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FF0081; color:black;"> </span> </span>  NACACAA – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_America,_Central_America_and_Caribbean_Athletic_Association" title="North America, Central America and Caribbean Athletic Association">North America, Central America and Caribbean Athletic Association</a></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#0081FF; color:black;"> </span> </span>  EAA – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_Athletics_Association" title="European Athletics Association">European Athletics Association</a></li>
<li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend nowrap"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#FFFF00; color:black;"> </span> </span>  OAA – <a href="/wiki/Oceania_Athletics_Association" title="Oceania Athletics Association">Oceania Athletics Association</a></li></ul>
<p>National level athletics organisations are responsible for the regulation of the sport within their respective countries and most major competitions have some form of permit or approval from their national body.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Competitions">Competitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Competitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1500_metres_men_final_Tampere_2018_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1050" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/1500_metres_men_final_Tampere_2018_%281%29.jpg/220px-1500_metres_men_final_Tampere_2018_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/1500_metres_men_final_Tampere_2018_%281%29.jpg/330px-1500_metres_men_final_Tampere_2018_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/1500_metres_men_final_Tampere_2018_%281%29.jpg/440px-1500_metres_men_final_Tampere_2018_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1500_metres_men_final_Tampere_2018_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Men's 1,500-metre Running during the <a href="/wiki/2018_IAAF_World_U20_Championships" title="2018 IAAF World U20 Championships">2018 IAAF World U20 Championships</a> at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ratina_Stadium" title="Ratina Stadium">Ratina Stadium</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tampere" title="Tampere">Tampere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a></div></div></div>
<p>.Athletics competitions can be broadly divided into three types: international championships, national championships, and annual meetings and races. Athletics at international championships, or Games, represent the pinnacle of competition within the sport, and they are contested between athletes representing their country or region. The organisation of these competitions is usually overseen by either a world, continental, or regional athletics governing body. Athletes gain entry into these competitions by earning selection from their national athletics governing body, which is generally done by assessing athletes via their past achievements or performances at a national selection event. National championships are annual competitions endorsed by a national governing body which serve the purpose of deciding the country's best athlete in each event. Annual one-day meetings and races form the most basic level of competition and are the most common format of athletics contests. These events are often invitational and are organised by sports organisations, sports promoters, or other institutions.
</p><p>Competitions typically feature only one of the sports within athletics. However, major outdoor international athletics championships and athletics competitions held as part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-sport_events" title="Multi-sport events">multi-sport events</a> usually feature a combination of track and field, road running and racewalking events
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_competitions">International competitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: International competitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Multi-sport_events">Multi-sport events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Multi-sport events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beijing_Olympic_Stadium_August_15_709713b52c_o.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Beijing_Olympic_Stadium_August_15_709713b52c_o.jpg/220px-Beijing_Olympic_Stadium_August_15_709713b52c_o.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Beijing_Olympic_Stadium_August_15_709713b52c_o.jpg/330px-Beijing_Olympic_Stadium_August_15_709713b52c_o.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Beijing_Olympic_Stadium_August_15_709713b52c_o.jpg/440px-Beijing_Olympic_Stadium_August_15_709713b52c_o.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beijing_Olympic_Stadium_August_15_709713b52c_o.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The athletics competition underway at the main stadium of the <a href="/wiki/2008_Summer_Olympics" title="2008 Summer Olympics">2008 Summer Olympics</a></div></div></div>
<p>The modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summer_Olympics" title="Summer Olympics">Summer Olympics</a> was the first event at which a global athletics competition took place. All the four major sports within athletics have featured in the <a href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_Summer_Olympics" title="Athletics at the Summer Olympics">Olympic athletics programme</a> since its inception in 1896, although cross country has since been dropped. The Olympic competition is the most prestigious athletics contest, and many athletics events are among the most watched events at the Summer Olympics. A total of 47 athletics events are held at the Olympics, 24 for men and 23 for women (as of London 2012). The events within the men's and women's programmes are either identical or have a similar equivalent, with the sole exception being that men contest the 50 km race walk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the model of the Olympics, various other <a href="/wiki/Multi-sport_event" title="Multi-sport event">multi-sport events</a> arose during the 20th century, which included athletics as a core sport within the programme from the outset. These included the <a href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_Commonwealth_Games" title="Athletics at the Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_Central_American_and_Caribbean_Games" title="Athletics at the Central American and Caribbean Games">Central American and Caribbean Games</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_Universiade" title="Athletics at the Universiade">Universiade</a>, and many others.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summer_Paralympics" title="Summer Paralympics">Summer Paralympics</a> include athletes with a <a href="/wiki/Physical_disability" title="Physical disability">physical disability</a>. Track and field, and road events have featured in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_Paralympic_Games" title="Athletics at the Paralympic Games">Paralympic athletics programme</a> since its inception in 1960. The Paralympic competition is the most prestigious athletics contest where athletes with a physical disability compete. Athletics at the Paralympic Games also include <a href="/wiki/Wheelchair_racing" title="Wheelchair racing">wheelchair racing</a> where athletes compete in lightweight racing chairs. Athletes with a <a href="/wiki/Visual_impairment" title="Visual impairment">visual impairment</a> compete with a <a href="/wiki/Sighted_guide" title="Sighted guide">sighted guide</a>. At the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Paralympics" title="2012 Summer Paralympics">2012 Summer Paralympics</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, for the first time at an international athletics event, the guides received medals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> such as the pilots in cycling, and the guides at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paralympic_Winter_Games" title="Paralympic Winter Games">Paralympic Winter Games</a> have done for a while.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_championships">World championships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: World championships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Athletics_Championships" title="World Athletics Championships">World Athletics Championships</a> is the primary global athletics championships held by World Athletics. The biennial competition was first held in 1983 and now features an event programme which is identical to the Olympics. Thus, road running, racewalking and track and field are the sports which feature at the competition. Cross country running has its own discrete global championships – the <a href="/wiki/World_Athletics_Cross_Country_Championships" title="World Athletics Cross Country Championships">World Athletics Cross Country Championships</a> – which has been held annually since 1973. The <a href="/wiki/World_Athletics_Indoor_Championships" title="World Athletics Indoor Championships">World Athletics Indoor Championships</a> is a biennial athletics championships which features solely indoor track and field events. The foremost separate road running event is the annual <a href="/wiki/World_Athletics_Half_Marathon_Championships" title="World Athletics Half Marathon Championships">World Athletics Half Marathon Championships</a> (formerly <i>IAAF World Half Marathon Championships</i>). While not having official world championship status, the biennial <a href="/wiki/World_Athletics_Race_Walking_Team_Championships" title="World Athletics Race Walking Team Championships">World Athletics Race Walking Team Championships</a> fulfils a similar role for the sport of racewalking. Outdoor track and field is the only sport in athletics that does not have a its own distinct global championship which is separate from other types of athletics, although the <a href="/wiki/IAAF_Continental_Cup" title="IAAF Continental Cup">IAAF Continental Cup</a> (a quadrennial competition between continental teams) is composed entirely of outdoor track and field events.
</p><p>Other world championships include the <a href="/wiki/World_Athletics_U20_Championships" title="World Athletics U20 Championships">World Athletics U20 Championships</a> and the 2017 defunct <a href="/wiki/IAAF_World_Youth_Championships_in_Athletics" title="IAAF World Youth Championships in Athletics">World Youth Championships in Athletics</a>, which are for athletes under-19 and under-17, respectively. <a href="/wiki/World_Masters_Athletics" title="World Masters Athletics">World Masters Athletics</a> conducts the <a href="/wiki/World_Masters_Athletics_Championships" title="World Masters Athletics Championships">World Masters Athletics Championships</a> for athletes in 5-year age divisions over the age of 35.  The now defunct <a href="/wiki/IAAF_World_Road_Relay_Championships" title="IAAF World Road Relay Championships">IAAF World Road Relay Championships</a> served as the global event for <a href="/wiki/Ekiden" title="Ekiden">ekiden</a> marathon relay races. 
</p><p>Elite athletes with a physical disability compete at the <a href="/wiki/World_Para_Athletics_Championships" title="World Para Athletics Championships">World Para Athletics Championships</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture_and_media">Culture and media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sport_of_athletics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Culture and media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pentathlon_athlets_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen_2637.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1241" data-file-width="1314" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Pentathlon_athlets_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen_2637.jpg/150px-Pentathlon_athlets_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen_2637.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Pentathlon_athlets_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen_2637.jpg/225px-Pentathlon_athlets_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen_2637.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Pentathlon_athlets_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen_2637.jpg/300px-Pentathlon_athlets_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen_2637.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pentathlon_athlets_Staatliche_Antikensammlungen_2637.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient Greek pottery showing the javelin and the discus throw</div></div></div>
<p>Athletics, and its athletes in particular, has been artistically depicted since ancient times – one of the surviving instances include runners and high jumpers in the motifs of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptian</a> tombs dating from 2250 BC. Athletics was much respected in Ancient Greece and the events within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_pentathlon" title="Ancient pentathlon">ancient pentathlon</a> provided inspiration for large statues such as the <a href="/wiki/Discobolus" title="Discobolus">Discobolus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Discophoros" title="Discophoros">Discophoros</a>, and for motifs on countless vase and <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> works. <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> discussed the significance of the pentathlon in his <a href="/wiki/Treatise" title="Treatise">treatise</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Rhetoric_(Aristotle)" title="Rhetoric (Aristotle)">Rhetoric</a></i> and reflected on the athlete aesthetic of the period: "a body capable of enduring all efforts, either of the racecourse or of bodily strength...This is why the athletes in the pentathlon are most beautiful".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Films about athletics are overwhelmingly focused on running events: the 1962 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Loneliness_of_the_Long_Distance_Runner_(film)" title="The Loneliness of the Long Distance Runner (film)">The Loneliness of the Long Distance Runner</a></i> (based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Loneliness_of_the_Long_Distance_Runner" title="The Loneliness of the Long Distance Runner">book of the same name</a>) explores cross country running as a means of escape. <i><a href="/wiki/Chariots_of_Fire" title="Chariots of Fire">Chariots of Fire</a></i>, perhaps one of the most well-known athletics films, is a fictionalised account of <a href="/wiki/Eric_Liddell" title="Eric Liddell">Eric Liddell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harold_Abrahams" title="Harold Abrahams">Harold Abrahams</a>'s chase for sprint <a href="/wiki/Gold_medal" title="Gold medal">gold medals</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Athletics_at_the_1924_Summer_Olympics" title="Athletics at the 1924 Summer Olympics">1924 Olympics</a>. Track and field has been the subject of American films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Personal_Best_(film)" title="Personal Best (film)">Personal Best</a></i> (1981) and <i><a href="/wiki/Across_the_Tracks" title="Across the Tracks">Across the Tracks</a></i> (1991). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biopic" title="Biopic">Biopics</a> are found within the genre, including <i><a href="/wiki/Prefontaine_(film)" title="Prefontaine (film)">Prefontaine</a></i> (regarding <a href="/wiki/Steve_Prefontaine" title="Steve Prefontaine">Steve Prefontaine</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Jim_Thorpe_%E2%80%93_All-American" title="Jim Thorpe – All-American">Jim Thorpe – All-American</a></i> (1951) featuring <a href="/wiki/Burt_Lancaster" title="Burt Lancaster">Burt Lancaster</a> as Thorpe. Documentaries are also common with examples such as 2007 film <i><a href="/wiki/Spirit_of_the_Marathon" title="Spirit of the Marathon">Spirit of the Marathon</a></i>, which follows runners' preparations for the 2005 <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Marathon" title="Chicago Marathon">Chicago Marathon</a>.
</p><p>Books on the subject are predominantly non-fiction, and tend towards the forms of training manuals and historical accounts of athletics. The story of the <a href="/wiki/Four-minute_mile" title="Four-minute mile">four-minute mile</a> has been a particularly popular subject, spawning books such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Perfect_Mile" title="The Perfect Mile">The Perfect Mile</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=3:59.4:_The_Quest_to_Break_the_Four_Minute_Mile&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="3:59.4: The Quest to Break the Four Minute Mile (page does not exist)">3:59.4: The Quest to Break the Four Minute Mile</a></i>.
</p><p>Athletics <a href="/wiki/Journalism" title="Journalism">journalism</a> has spawned a number of dedicated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodical" title="Periodical">periodicals</a> including <i><a href="/wiki/Athletics_Weekly" title="Athletics Weekly">Athletics Weekly</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Race_Walking_Record" title="Race Walking Record">Race Walking Record</a></i>, both of which were first published in England in the early 1940s, and <i><a href="/wiki/Track_%26_Field_News" title="Track &amp; Field News">Track &amp; Field News</a></i> which was first published in the United States in 1948. <i><a href="/wiki/Runner%27s_World" title="Runner's World">Runner's World</a></i> has been in print since 1966 and the <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Track_%26_Field_Magazine_of_Japan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Track &amp; Field Magazine of Japan (page does not exist)">Track &amp; Field Magazine of Japan</a></i> (<i>Rikujyo Kyogi Magazine</i>) is another long-running publication.
</p><p>Athletics events have been selected as a main motif in numerous collectors' coins.  One of the recent samples is the €10 Greek <a href="/wiki/Euro_gold_and_silver_commemorative_coins_(Greece)#2003_coinage" title="Euro gold and silver commemorative coins (Greece)">Running commemorative coin</a>, minted in 2003 to commemorate the <a href="/wiki/2004_Summer_Olympics" title="2004 Summer Olympics">2004 Summer Olympics</a>. In the obverse of the coin, a modern athlete figure appears in the foreground, shown in the starting position, while in the background two ancient runners are carved in a manner that gives the appearance of a coin that is "worn" by time. This scene originally appeared on a black-figure vase of the 6th century BC.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
